PLACES A BURDEN
ON THE TEACHERS
The New Charter Will Curtail the Amount of
MoQey Available for t^e Schools.
Any matter which touches the public schools Is of greneral interest and
concern- No scheme which tended In any measure to cripple them would
be regarded with patience. The teachers themselves constitute a lar^e
and necessarily intelligent class whose desires are worthy of considera
tion. In the discussion of the proposed charter it Is therefore necessary and
proper to give full weight to the effect its adoption would have upon the
schools; whether It would tend to cripple them or add to their power and
usefulness.
The table presented below is complied by Cyril Williams, an expert,
whose familiarity with the subject will give to his figures a peculiar force.
It demonstrates that the provisions of the new charter will place a burden
upon the teachers, curtail the amount of money available for their ser
vices, and cause either a reduction in the number of teachers
or a reduction in the pay they now receive, which has not heretofore been
deemed too great. The table will bear careful study. It tells Its own story:
SCHOOL, FUND.
Appropriation for the fiscal year 1897-98, for maintaining public 5ch0015.. 51,200,000 00
Out of this appropriation was paid "back salaries of the previous fiscal
year amounting to 120,000 00
Leaving for current expenses a balance of $1,080,000 00
This appropriation was derived from the following sources:
From taxation $ 518,200 00
From the State and other sources 681,800 00
Total $1,200,000 00
The new charter, article VII, chapter 5, section 2, provides:
"Shall levy for the Common School fund a tax which, added to the
revenue derived from other sources, shall produce an amount of money
which shall not exceed thirty-two dollars and fifty cents for each pupil in
attendance during the preceding fiscal year."
The average daily attendance during the year ending June 30, 1597,
was 33,631 at $32 50 per pupil $1,089,767 00
(See Municipal Reports 1596-97. page 1152.)
The appropriation for current expenses for fiscal year 1897-98, as
shown above, was : $1,080,000 00
SCHOOL FUND SALARIES.
The new charter, article VII, chapter 3, section 9, reads:
"To provide for the prompt payment on not later than the fifth day
of every month of all salaries due and allowed officers, teachers and
other employes of the School Department. For this purpose the Auditor
shall annually segregate so much of the Common School fund as shall
not exceed twenty-eight dollars for each pupil in average daily attend
ance in the Public Schools of the city and county during the preceding
fiscal year."
The average daily attandance during the year ending June 30, 1897,
was 33.531 at $2S per pupil $ 938,868 00
The full amount allowed for all salaries at present, the superin
tendent, superintendent's deputy, superintendent's secretary, are not
paid out of the school fund, but out of the general fund, on an additional
appropriation; therefore deduct ihe salaries of the corresponding and
additional officers under the new charter, to-wit:
Four directors at $3.< Xx) $12,000
One superintendent at. 4,000 4.000
Four deputies at 2.4'J0 9.600
One secretary at 1,800 1,800
Total 27,400 00
Which leaves a balance of $ 911,468 00
This brings the amount to the basis on which salaries are now paid
out of the school fund.
For the month of April, 1597, the salaries paid were as follows:
Teachers, etc $58,976 00
Shop employes 602 50
Office employes 832 50
Total $90,410 00
â¢For twelve months these salaries would amount to $1,084,920 00
This shows that the adoption of the new charter will make an annual
reduction in the salaries of teachers, clerks, etc., of $ 173.452 00
GUARDING THE
CITY OF PEKING
Eighteen Men From the
Monterey on Duty at
the Steamer.
Almost a Riot Among the
Men Who Went North
on the Sintram.
Threa Men Kllied Â«nd One Died of
Natural Causes During thÂ«
Roanoke's Voyage.
The naval authorities are taking no
' chances with anything that will be put
aboard the City of Pektng. The big
steamer came down from Hunters Point
, dry dock Thursday afternoon, and yester
: day, before the work of putting stores on
her was begun, a guard of eighteen men
; was sent to her from the Monterey. Six
! of Uncle Sam's sailors were put in the
; lighters alongside the big liner loaded
with coal, six were placed on the dock
> to keep watch on all who went aboard
and six were stationed in the hold in
specting every bucket of coal as it was
dumped. The men keep an eight-hour
watch and then go back to the Monterey.
They do not like the job and would much
rather be fighting the Spanish than get
ting as dirty as blackamoors with coal
dust instead of with powder smoke.
Whether they like it or not, however,
does not make any difference, as they
will have to keep watch on the Peking
until the vessel is ready to sail.
The Peking will carry as many troops
, as she has accommodations for. No extra
; berths will be provided and the vessel
will get away the early part of the week.
â  The City of Sydney will have extra ac
! commodations put in the 'tween decks
and steerage and the Australia has al
most been fitted out in that manner. The
latter vessel will come down from the
i Union Iron Works to-day and when
docked at the Oceanic dock she will be
; practically ready to take aboard the
troops and supplies. If any precautions
are taken regarding the loading of the
. Australia, City of Sydney, Ohio and Con
emaugb, the guards will be soldiers, as
i these vessels have been chartered by
i the army authorities, while the Peking
is under charter of the Secretary of the
: Navy. The authorities are in hopes of
; getting all five vessels away within the
| next week or ten days.
The sailors on the Monterey are discon
solate. Big brown Jumbo, the ship's
mascot, has deserted. He was given
shore leave a few days ago and failed to
show up when his time had expired. Yes
terday a corporal's guard was searching
the highways and byways of the water
front in search of the runaway. Jumbo
is the dog that saved Â»he life of one of
i the crew a few days ago, and the men in
! consequence feel all the more the loss of
their pet. Anybody who runs across a
big brown dog with a collar bearing th<* j
name of the coast defense steamer, and
who will return him to the coxswain of
the Monterey's launch, will receive the
thanks of the entire ship's crew.
The schooner Hattie 1. Phillips sailed i
for St. Michael yesterday with two par- |
ties of gold hunters aboard. She was i
the remains of the Gloucester Mining !
Company, who came out from the East
on the schooner. They have with them !
the launch Eclipse, and on their arrivnl
at St Michael will turn the vessel over
to the agents of Cassidy & Snow, her
new owners. The other party is the Sun- j
flower Mining Company of Fort Scott. |
Kans and they are taking along with '
them'the launch Sunflower.

THE ODD FELLOWS.
They Will Maintain the Home at
Thermalitoâ Members of the
Order May Go to War.
At the morning session of the Grand
Lodge of Odd Fellows yesterday Past
Grand Louderback was given a vote of
thanks for his digest of Grand Lodge
laws.
D. Newell of this city was elected trus
tee of the home at Thermalito.
Subordinates were permitted to make
donations to keep in good standing mem
bers who have enlisted or may enlist for
the war. \ '
The charter of Ontario Lodge, which
had been revoked for insubordination, was
restored upon the presentation of satis
factory explanations. â 
A long discussion was had during the
afternoon on the subject of the home at
Thermalito, and the grand master was
authorized to appoint a committee of five
which during the coming year shall thor
oughly examine Into the manner of con
ducting the home and its cost and make
a report at the next Grand Lodge session.
It was decided to sustain the home and
provide for its maintenance.
W. A. Bruton ; representative from
Grass Valley, was taken ill during the
session, and was removed to St. Lultte's
Hospital.
In the evening there was a session to
adopt the several reports presented.

FEEE DAY AT HOPKINS.
Bain and Murky Weather Keep the
Picture Lovers Away.
Yesterday was free day at the Hopkins
Art Institute. Contrary to custom and
on account of the portrait exhibit, the
second Friday of the month instead of the
first, was given over to the general pub
lic. But few, however, took advantage
of the privilege, the soft May rain and
general murky weather contributing, no
doubt, to keep the picture-loving publio
away.
The Htriking posters of the portrait ex
hibit, which are still In place, were great
ly admired, though much regret was ex
pressed that the general public were not
privileged to enjoy the elegant collection
of portraits and rare old china of last
week.

TO REMEDY A
GREAT ABUSE
Mr. Loud's Course Ap
proved by Secretary
Richardson.
Congress Should Investigate
the Cause of the Large An
nual Deficiency.
Advertising Periodical* Take Ad
vantage of the Second Class
Privileges.
The postal authorities of this city do
not take kindly to the proposition recent
ly made In "Washington that the number
of dally mall deliveries in the large busi
ness centers of the United States should
be cut down in order to reduce the alarm
ing deficiency in the Postoffice Depart
ment, which has existed for many years.
There are five mall deliveries per day
In San Francisco in the business districts,
and should this number be cut down to
four much hardship and inconvenience
will be inflicted upon the business com
munity. Daniel S. Richardson, secretary
for the postoffice In this city, said yes
terday that San Francisco merchants
had become so much accustomed to the
five daily deliveries that the lopping off
of even one of them would cause them
much inconvenience and would injurious
ly affect the business interests of the en
tire community.
The postal deficiency, he said, was large
ly due to the abuse of the second class
mail privilege. The mails are clogged, he
said, with serial novels and other trashy
publications. The class of literature
which gets the benefit of the second class
privilege is the lower order of literature
anu in no case the higher. Among- the
mass are a large number of papers
claiming second class rates that are pri
marily designed for advertising and free
olrcuiation in hotels and on railway
trains, and a class of publications repre
senting certain trades and industries. For j
instance, a grocery firm will issue a cat- ]
alogue of prices and add something to it j
to make it Just come within the require
ments of second class matter, thus oblig
ing the Government to distribute its ad
vertising. If these catalogues were
obliged to pay third class rates the firm
would pay eight cents a pound instead of
one cent a pound, which is the second
class rate.
"In addition to the abuses of the sec
ond-class privilege my impression is,"
continued Mr. Kichardson, "that the !
rates paid by the Government are !
too high for the transportation of i
mail matter in proportion to the rates of !
freight charged by transportation com- !
panles for other freight and requiring â¢
similar care. If the second-class privi- j
lege were restrictpd to legitimate newspa- j
I pers and periodicals the deficiency would ;
be speedily wiped out. The privilege
should be restricted and the rates puid
for transportation shouid be reduced."
Mr. Richardson showed some interest- j
ing statistics bearing on the matter. Dur- i
Ing 1896 there was a revenue to the Gov- I
ernment of $60,624,464 on 65,337,343 pounds i
of first-class matter. The revenue dur- \
ing the same year on 348,988,G4S pounds of I
second-class matter was $2,966,403. ,
The revenue on 7&.701.14S pounds of |
third-class matter was $10,324,0fi9, and
the revenue from 19,950,187 pounds of
fourth-class matter or merchandise was !
$3,129,321. From the years ISB7 to 1896, in
clusive, the total cost of transportation !
of second-class matter amounting to 2,097,- ;
640.300 pounds was $167.511.224, while the re- !
celpts to the Government to pay for the ;
same were only $20,y76,403. leaving a de- |
flclency of $146,834,821. It should be bornÂ© |
in nrtnd that this deficiency is for the i
transportation of second-class matter
only.
Mr. Richardson called attention to an
other important factor in the case, and
that was the fact that the Government
was getting all the long-haul matter and
the express companies are getting the !
advantage of all the concentrations of
weight and short-haul matter. Within
a radius of 500 miles the express com- j
panics are carrying second-class matter
for a fraction under a cent per pound.
Mr. Richardson added that the business
community were under a d<Â»bt of grati
tude to The Call for the stand it has ta
ken m this matter of reforming the abuse
which 1b responsible for the great defi
ciency. The editorial In yesterday morn- j
Ing's Issue of The Call was timely and to \
the point. A congressional investigation
should be had by all means.

comfortable person. She is illogical,
I unhappy and frequently hysterical.
The condition of the mind known as
" the bines," nearly always, with wo-
men, results from diseased organs of
generation.

: age of advanced medical science, any
! person should still believe that mere
force of will and determination will
overcome depressed spirits and nerv-
ousness in women. These troubles are
i indications of disease.
Every woman who doesn't trader-
i stand her condition should write to
j Lynn, Mass., to Mrs. Pinkham for her
â  advice. Her advice is thorough com-

learned woman of great experience'

TriE FORTUNE OF WAR.
WHILE the voices of the people were still
sounding with glad acclamations over the
gallant feat of the Winslow on Monday in at
tacking single-handed three Spanish gunboats and
driving them to seek shelter under the guns of the
forts of Cardenas Bay, came the news of yesterday
announcing another daring feat of the same boat.
another victory for our flag, but gained at a fearful
cost to the vessel and her crew. The Winslow, badly
wrecked, lies in port awaiting repairs, and the greater
number of the brave men who so courageously won
fame for her are dead or wounded.
The service on which the Winslow was sent that
led to her destruction was of so venturesome a na
ture that at first reading it will seem to the country
that some one must have blundered. A careful study
of the situation, however, shows that such was not
the case. In sea fights as well as in land fights peril
is not to be avoided. There are times when a few
men must perform deeds of seeming recklessness in
order that some great benefit or advantage may be
gained for the army or the fleet which they serve.
Such an emergency occurred in the contest at Carr
denas Bay.
It is clearly wise on the part of our commanders to
destroy as many as possible of the Spanish war ves
sels now in West Indian waters before the main fleet
of Spain can unite with them. Several of these .Span
ish gunboats were in the bay at Cardenas. They con
stituted to our blockade and our operations in those
waters a menace which it was necessary to remove.
It was known that the principal entrances to the bay
were mined and that for our ships to steam boldly
in would be to invite the destruction of the entire
squadron. Another entrance had to be sought out,
and as a tnatter of course the vessel that led the way
had to be of comparatively small draught lest she
should strand upon the shoals.
The little Winslow was chosen to lead the way.
She found a passage deep enough for the tug Wilson
and the gunboat Hamilton, but the larger ships of
the squadron could not follow. The three boats held
on their course, found the Spanish boats at anchor
under the guns of the land batteries and at once at
tacked them. The feat was on a lesser scale but not
less daring than that of Dewey at Manila. The men
of the Winslow died not recklessly, but gloriously.
The occasion is one over which patriotism may exult
for the valor shown as well as mourn for the heroic
dead.
This is the fortune of war. The American seaman
drawing his inspiration from a long line of heroes
from the days of Paul Jones to those of Farragut,
and roused to emulation by the marvelous victory
achieved at Manila, cannot turn his back on any foe
however strong he may appear when the path of duty
leads straight forward. It is inevitable that at times
disasters befall the brave. Were it otherwise it
would not require bravery tc face the chances of war.
This first blow given to our arms in the struggle with
Spain warns us that it is not a holiday task our sol
diers and seamen have entered upon, but it will also
remind us that no duty it imposes will be too perilous
for our heroes to meet and overcome.
Somebody was certainly at fault when the volun
teers from Oregon were not met at the station and
given military escort. They were a gallant looking
lot; they have become soldiers for love of country,
and though they come from beyond the State line
they are all our boys just the same. There are more
of them to come, and the rest should not be slighted.
A correspondent sends to a New York paper the
story of the Srui Juan battle in which he speaks of the
women fleeing in yellow fear. Had he been showing
the unfortunate ladies a copy of the paper for which
lie writes details of things which never have hap
pened?
The man who sues for divorce alleging that his
wife took but one bath in five months should remem
ber that the water company has earnestly requested
citizens to be as sparing as possible of water.
Certain of the powers are said still to be deter
mined that the United States shall not hold the Phil
ippines. Spain can be counted among the number.
It cannot be denied that the volunteers sent up a
sigh of relief when they learned that it was another
Otis.
The Spanish fleet now in search of Sampson will be
sorry when it shall find him, .
THE FUN OF FUSION.
1-"HE first real political fusion of the year has been
effected at Fresno. In that county the Demo
crats, Silver Republicans and Populists have
struck palms and agreed to make a joint fight on a
common platform. The first knowledge gained by
the outside world of this Fresno compact is in the re
port made to the Populist State Committee by Mr.
Dore, who seems to have been its principal nego
tiator.
In the first place the Democrats agree not to have any
Democratic platform. This is an abstention that must
cost many a pang, for if there is any privilege dear to
the Democratic heart it is that of passing resolutions
and making State, county, township and precinct plat
forms. The Democrats agree to eat their tag, which
is a genuine hardship. The Silver Republicans never
had any platform or principles except a declaration
that the tail is bigger than the dog and should wag
him whenever it is fed. These two members of the
political happy family of Fresno agree to fuse with the
Populists on the Oregon platform, and, having no
other, to cleave unto that until death does them part.
Now the Oregon platform is the Populist platform.
The Democrats of Oregon fused with -the Populists
once and lost their identity. The Populists went to
the front, and in the so-called fusion now in operation
in that State they said to the few and scattering bands
of Democrats who had strayed so far fr&m their own
reservation that they couldn't find the way back that
they could take a seat inside the Populist lion by sub
scribing to the regular platform. As they had no re
course, they accepted the conditions. On this plat
form the Fresno fusion is made. It is the full Popu
list programme. It demands "a national money, safe
and sound, issued by the General Government only,
without the intervention of banks of issue, to be a full
legal tender for all debts, public and private; also a
just, equitable and efficient means of distribution di-
rect to the people through the lawful disbursements of
the Government, and that the volume of circulating
medium be speedily increased to an amount suffi
cient to meet the demands of business and population
of this country, and to restore the just level of prices;
and such legislation as will prevent, in the future, de
monetization of any kind of legal tender money by
private contract." The platform also demands the
initiative and referendum and the submission of "all
important national questions for an advisory vote of
the people." ,
The Populists were not content with the adminis
tration of this dose to the Fresno Democrats. Mr.
Dore reported to the State Committee that after the
Democrats and Silver Republicans had agreed to the
platform "a fair division of the offices was arrived at,
the Populists, however, stipulating for control of the
Board of Supervisors and of the offices governing
the levying of taxes and the expenditure of public
moneys."
The humor of this is exquisite. The Democrats
seem not to have seen it. Though they took their
platform medicine the Populists deemed it wise not
to trust them with money, and so stipulated for
control of the purse. But, after all, this is wise poli
tics. If the Populists hang on to the bait-can, the
other parties to this funny compact must wig when
they wag.
THE DISMISSED GRAND JURY.
THE Grand Jury dismissed by Judge Belcher
yesterday for incompetency is not likely to get
much sympathy from the public. The distress
of its members is of an eminently lachrymose char
acter. If a few of the desperate resolves with which
they are now acquainting the public had been exe
cuted while the jury were investigating the ferry
depot scandal, the School Department thefts and the
street paving frauds the disgraceful ending which has
overtaken them might have been prevented. Several
of the jurors say they were on the point of doing
something. It is a significant fact, however, that they
never were on this point until Judge Belcher an
nounced his intention of dismissing them.
It is childish for the jurors to charge District At
torney Barnes and his assistants with responsibility
for their failure to do their duty. Had they been de
termined, for instance, to indict the thieving contract
ors who robbed the State in constructing the ferry
depot â proof of whose criminality was presented in
abundance â neither the District Attorney nor his as
sistants could have averted the result. Had they been
intent on bringing the School Department rascals to
justice no power on earth could have stopped them.
Moreover, nobody would have attempted to stop them.
The bravest District Attorney or Superior Judge who
ever served the tax eaters would not have dared to
stand between an honest Grand Jury and the political
thieves of the Harbor Commission and School De
partment.
The people of this city will not be apt to credit what
is now being said concerning the desperate inten
tions of the jurors. Josh Biilings said he never
wagered a cent on a man who told what he would
have done had he been there. Nor will the public lay
any money on Grand Jurors who tell what they would
have done had Judge Belcher let them alone a while
longer. The fact of the matter is the dismissed Grand
Jury, although in session for six months, did nothing.
If its inactivity had been due to a lack of material to
work on, there would have been no occasion to de
mand its dismissal. But political thieves have been
unusually abundant for a long time. A finer oppor
tunity for the display of civic virtue was never pre
sented to a Grand Jury than has been presented to
this one. Yet its members completely failed to do
their duty.
What the disgraced jurors should do is to retire
from public view quietly. Anything they may now
say can only vex the public ear. They had a chance
to purify the political atmosphere and they neglected
it. What they might have done, what they were
going to do, why they did not do it, and who was to
blame for the internecine difficulties, are no longer of
public interest. The historical fact is, they quarreled
when they should have attended to business. The
"pull" of the predatory politicians was too much for
them. It is well that they are dead and buried.
THE SACRAMENTO WATER SUPPLY.
STEPS taken by the citizens of Sacramento and
by the State officials to procure a purer water
supply for Sacramento are a striking illustration
of- the benefits accruing to a community from a
prompt publication of existing evils and an insistent
urging of the adoption of an immediate remedy.
When The Call first exposed the dangerous condi
tion of the water supply at Sacramento the exposure
was met by the charge that it was an attempt to in
jure the city. Certain of our contemporaries started
the charge and tried hard to raise it to the degree of
a prolonged howl. It takes more than two coyotes,
however, to make noise enough to distract attention
from the clear voice of The Call, and the people of
Sacramento were not for a moment deceived. They
knew that the articles on the water supply were true,
THE SA^ FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, MAY 14, 1898.
and that the object of The Call in publishing them
was not to injure Sacramento, but to save her peo
ple from danger of disease.
The first result of the publication was the awaken
ing of popular interest in the subject. This led to
discussion, and discussion brought about investiga
tion. All the statements made by The Call were
found to be true. The next step was to devise a
remedy. Men of intelligence and energy took up the
work, and as soon as Jhey began to move it was found
they would have ample co-operation from the State
officials in checking the evil so far as it is due to the
contamination of the water by the sewage from Fol-
som prison-
It was gratifying to note by the reports from Sac
ramento yesterday that the movement for a better
water supply has now so far progressed that a victory
for Sacramento is virtually assured. It is regarded as
certain that the Prison Directors at their next meet
ing will provide some way by which the sewage of
Folsom can be cared for without draining it into
American River. This will be a gain not for Sacra
mento only, but for all the region along the rivers be
low the prison, and may serve as a means for pro
moting sewage improvement in many other locali
ties.
Sacramento is to be congratulated upon the energy
of her Chamber of Commerce and her citizens gener
ally in dealing with the issue. The importance of the
work to be undertaken cannot be overrated. An im
pure water supply is a menace to every household in
the city. It is one of the most fruitful causes of dis
ease among men, and if the present impure supply
were continued there is no telling what a fearful cost
it might have entailed upon the people of Sacra
mento in the coming summer. Fortunately the evil
is to be no longer ignored. The remedy has been
devised and agreed upon. Sacramento will soon be
as healthful as nature made that locality before man
contaminated the pure waters of the rivers.
COUNSEL TO THE ERRING.
THERE is no longer need for considering the
Examiner seriously. From a newspaper it has
degenerated into a serial freak, to whose opinion
not the slightest importance attaches and whose
"news" no reader regards as indicative of anything
that has happened or is likely to happen. But a
freak becomes tiresome; the side-show and the voice
of the spieler pall. Would it not be well for the Ex
aminer when it has published as truth statements
having not the coloring of fact to refrain from
boasting? The public is ready to assume that the
verbose liars who represent yellow journalism
abroad will keep up their regular lick, especially as
the present affords no end of opportunity.
Recently a man named Creelman â as a correspond
ent utterly unreliable â sent word that he had broken
through the Spanish censorship and began to forward
"interviews" which all knew to be fictitious. Then
he announced that the fleet which Sampson had ex
pected to meet had returned to Cadiz. He let the
public know that he had given the United States the
benefit of this knowledge, and that Uncle Sam was
duly grateful. The location of the fleet was named
definitely, not as a rumor, a possibility, but as a matter
settled and certain. Yet it was an absolute lie, wrong
in itself and doubly wrong that some official might
have credited it and been led to disastrous course.
Happily the lie was discovered in time to destroyany
power for ill. But when news came to all the papers
showing the real character of the Creelman fake the
Examiner printed such of it as limited facilities per
mitted it to obtain, and at the same time bragged of
the achievement the real news stamped as a stupid
and malign fabrication. Such is the manner of it.
Its errors are not of ignorance wholly. The same
ambition which surges in the breast of a gold-brick
sharp every time he spies a greenhorn stirs the great
heart of the Examiner when a nickel is in sight. There
is no need to waste sympathy on the owner of the
nickel who permits it thus to be wooed away. There
is no use to advise the sheet to reform, but if it were
to cease by displays of red fire and blare of horn to
call attention to its own frailties it would abate a
nuisance and confer a considerable boon.
A GUN ASSEMBLING PLANT.
A PETITION has been addressed to the Senators
and Representatives of California in Congress
requesting them to use their best efforts to
bring about the establishment of a gun-assembling
plant for the Pacific Coast at Benicia. The petition
has been signed not only by leading citizens of
Benicia, but by such representative organizations as
the State Board of Trade, the Chamber of Commerce,
the Merchants' Association and the Manufacturers'
Association. It is therefore one that will compel at
tention by reason of the influential men who support
it, and our delegation in Congress can be safely
counted on to do what they can to obtain a favor
able consideration of it by the Government.
The petition, however, has a strength of its own
over and above that given it by the eminence of the
men and organizations who have signed it. The rea
sons why a gun-assembling plant should be estab
lished on this coast are many and all are strong. The
Pacific Coast frontage of the republic has ceased to
be the back door ol the nation. The whole trend of
events of recent years is toward the extension of
wealth, commerce and industry along these shores.
It is reasonably certain that within a comparatively
short time the principal energies of the nations will
be exerted on the Pacific. Our country must prepare
a strong front here where it will have to meet the ex
panding commerce and naval and military energies
of Russia, Germany, Great Britain and Japan. This
coast, equally with that of the Atlantic, will have to
be defended, and for defensive purposes there will be
required every form of plant known to modern war.
When it comes to the selection of a site for the lo
cation of a gun-assembling plant the claims of
Benicia cannot be overlooked. That point is ideally
situated for 6uch an establishment It holds a cen
tral position along the coast, is so landlocked as to
be safe from the attack of an enemy, and at the same
time is so directly connected with the ocean as to
make it a place where either the navy or coast defense
troops can promptly resort for guns when needed.
Further arguments for the establishment of the pro
posed plant at Benicia are to be found in the fact that
the Government has already there a suitable site for
the purpose. The reservation at Benicia includes 350
acres bordering on the waters of Carquinez Straits
at a point where the waters are' of sufficient depth at
low tide to afford ample facilities for the docking or
anchorage of the largest war vessels. On this site the
Government has now extensive barracks and shops.
It is within five miles of the navy yard at Mare
Island and possesses every advantage which could be
desired.
The petition should be strongly pushed. It is one
on which all Californians can unite. We need a gun
assembling plant on this coast, and Benicia is the
place for it. Moreover, it should be established at
once. The Government should act without delay.

The household
of a certain Mr.
Dunham of this
city was made
happy a short
time aaro by the
arrival of a little
NAMED
THE BABY
DEWEY.
0000000000 stranger in tTie
person of a healthy, bouncing boy, and
when the baby was christened the father,
who felt a true American pride In the
brilliant achievement of Admiral Dewey
over the Spanish fleet, to evince his pa
triotic spirit had the youngster named
Dewey, in honor of the illustrious de
fender of our nation's honor.
Yesterday afternoon Mr. Dunham had
occasion to go to the Occidental Hotel on
business, when he was agreeably sur
prised to meet an old friend who had just
arrived from the East. After mutual
greetings, he was on the point of inform
ing him of the new addition to his family
when the other commenced to speak in
glowing terms of the splendid American
victory in the Philippines, Admiral Dewey
especially coming in for a large share of
his admiration. Both gentlemen being of
an equally patriotic nature, the conversa
tion naturally became very animated, and
it was some time before Mr. Dunham was
able to venture the remark, "Well, we've
got a new boy up at our house." Con
gratulations were immediately extended.
"Yes," continued the proud father, at the
thought of the pleasant event, "Dewey
Dunham," meaning, of course, to convey
to his friend the name of the lad and his
own patriotic action in naming the baby.
The other individual, whose thoughts had
by that time again reverted to their orig-
inal conversation on the chastisement ad
ministered to the Spaniards, replied: "You
are right, my boy, dead right, even if you
are a little ungrammatical. You know, it
would be more proper to say, 'Dewey
did 'em.' "

NAMING THE BICYCLE.
Both the velocipede and bicycle have
given the French a lot of trouble to sat
isfactorily christen either machine. Be
ginning with the celerifere and veloslfere,
there has been a progressive movement
in the direction of "bicycle," "bicyclette."
"veloce" and "velo." The popular vehicle
1b also known under the name of "be
cane." In Spain It Is the veloclfers, ve
lodpedo and biclcletta. In England It Is
"wheel"; In Germany, "fahrrad or "rod."
In China It is "gaugma," meaning "for
eign horse," or "fei chal." "flying vm
chine." In the same country it is also
called "tzu tzun," or "carriage-that-goes
without-horse." With respect to names,
however, the Flemings distance all the
rest. Beginning by calling the bicycle
the "enelwiel" or "voetwiel," they have
developed the name into the mon
strous "gewielsnelrijuoetrappeudneusdre
kergestel. Those contemplating a bicy
cle tour in Europe had better skip Flan
ders or else ride around the country.

THE JEWS AS PATRIOTS.
Three thousand Jewish residents of Chi
cago on Saturday last gathered together
and took part in one of the most unique
demonstrations of patriotism In the
States since the war with Spain began.
In the Hebrew language, and according
to the forms of Moses, prayers were of
fered in the Jewish Tabernacle for the
protection and guidance of President Mc-
Kinley, for the success of our arms in
the warfare with Spain, and for the con
tinued welfare and prosperity of tho
United States Government. The exercises
were conducted by Rev. Israel Kupfer,
rabbi of the congregation, but the prin
cipal address was by William Zoloktkoff.
He declared that it was a peculiar and
providential happening that in a land of
liberty the Jews assembled- as respected
citizens of a great nation to pray for the
success of the arms of a nation that had
persecuted their ancestors.
"The persecution of the Jew," he said, '
"was the beginning of the decline of
Spain. To-day Spain ia tottering to ex
tinction, while the people she sought to
destroy are hopeful and strong. The Jew
who enjoys the freedom of this country
is willing to give his life to extend that
freedom to the possessions of Spain in !
this hemisphere, and he will rejoice in i
the downfall of the nation that once
drove his people from her shores."
Such demonstration as this will go far
toward setting at naught the absurd
assertion so often heard that "the Jews
are a nation without a country." It is |
very hard for some people to understand i
that Judaism in this day is a religion,
nothing more, nothing less. The Jews
are not a separate nation. The Jews of
America are as essentially American
citizens and are quite as patriotic and
devoted to the interests of the country as
the adherents of any other religion. â
Richmond (Va.) Star.
INVENTIONS BY NAVAL OFFICERS.
Under the prevailing custom. in regard
to the use by the Government of inven
tions by naval officers, the Secretary of
the Navy has hitherto allowed compen
sation In such cases, the amount being
fixed pursuant to recommendations of ex
pert naval officials. The clause of the
naval apropriation bill which the House
has refused to accept provided that naval |
officers holding patents on any discovery
or Invention used by the Government
might go into the Court of Claims and sue :
for compensation. The orderly and gen- i
erally satisfactory adjustment of such i
claims by the chief of the Navy Depart- '
ment would thus give place to a series of
artinns in court, with the Government
practically in the position of defendant ;
and one of its employes, nurtured and
educated at the public expense, as plain- !
tiff.
It is clear that the greater proportion of
inventions and discoveries useful in naval
practice must be the output of men spe
cially trained and adapted for naval duty.
Being thoroughly familiar with the con- i
ditions of the problems presented they j
have been enabled frequently to work out !
satisfactory solutions where the most I
learned of landlubbers would have groped â 
vainly in the dark.
In regard to the right of compensation >
for inventions by naval officers of de- )
vices and improvements advantageous to !
the service the rule of the department hai> I
always seen that when officers have
beÂ«*n specially assigned to experimental
duty, with special facilities afforded to
them by ; he department, the product of |
their investigations and experiments is
the property of the Government.
But if an invention for the naval ser
vice be devised by an officer as a result
of independent effort and personal enter
prise, he is entitled to fuli ownership of
the fruits of his labors, secured to nim j
by letters patent. The Government must
pay royalty for the use of such an inven
tion, although in all probability the only
customer for it. ,
If naval officers are to be paid for im
provements in naval construction, arma
ment and equipment, due provision might j
be easily made for such payment with- i
out resorting to the courts or to the
duress of a rider upon an appropriation I
bill.â Philadelphia Record.
BOMBARDMENT RULES.
Under the rules of war which have been
approved by all civilized nations, there
are certain things which a belligerent
cannot do without laying .himself liable
to the charge of barbarism.
There are certain rules which must be j
followed in the bombardment of cities.
According to Theodore S. Wolsey, a rec- j
ognized authority on international law, i
great consideration for persons and prop- j
erty must be displayed in a bombard
ment. All pillage is unlawful, however
protonged the resistance; unnecessary
destruction of property Is forbidden: >
quarter must be given, and killing must
be stopped as soon as resistance ceases.
Churches, hospitals, establishments de- i
voted to charity, the arts or to scientific ,
use should be marked by flags and spared
as much as practicable. Notice that a
bombardment is intended Is usually given, j
that non-combatants may be sheltered !
from its effects. Moreover, it is only â¢
fortified places that can be shelled. For
a hostile fleet to bombard a city that was
undefended would be an atrocious viola
tion of the laws of war.
While Spain would not hesitate to resort"
to atrocities, it Is not likely that she i
would violate the rules of war in such a i
way as to make herself an outlaw among
nations. For that reason, even if our i
fleet should not Intercept the Spanish j
men-of-war that are believed to bo headed
this way, there is little danger of the
bombardment of any unprotected city on
our coast.â From the Cleveland Leader.
AMERICAN ECONOMY OF TIME.
When visiting the United States, Have
lock Wilson was particularly Impressed
with the labor-saving machinea in opera
tion in tho different factories as well as
with the order and method with which
the works are conducted. In making a
tour around one factory in Ohio, he was
surprised to find above each machine a
huge placard, with the words, "Tho Hon.
J. Havelock Wilson, M. P.. from Eng
land." Asked why this notice was neces
sary, the manager said, "Oh. it is purely
a business arrangement. By placing this
notice in the works each man knows who
is the visitor being- conducted around, and
does not need to ask his mates. Time is
thus saved." Mr. Wilson also observed
that the men were allowed to sit at their
work. The manager said that was also
a business matter. "We find that the men
can do 15 per cent more work seated than
standing."

A WIFE'S PROPERTYâ C. W.. City.
The separate property of a wife, accord
ing to the law of this State, is not liable
for the debts of her husband, but is lia
ble for her own debts contracted before
or after marriage. As the husband Is the
head of the family, whatever necessaries
of life are procured for the family are in
law procured by him, and he Is responsi
ble for the payment thereof.
BARBERRYâ G. G. S., Pescadero. Cal.
The barberry common, on this coast is
known by the botanical name Barberideae
Aquofolium, and is also known as Oregon
Grape. The bark, roots and wood are
used for dyeing yellow. It produces v
bitter tonic, astringent; in large doses is
a cathartic. It is used locally in con
junctivus and internally in malarial and
typhoid fever. This department is unable
to state what effect its constant use
would have on the human system.

west by the Pacific Ocean and Alaska: on
the north by the Arctic Ocean and Alas
ka, on the south, southeast and south
west by the United States.

month.

POLLTAX COLLECTIONSâ M.. City.
There is no law that entitles corpora
tions or firms to a percentage for collect
ing polltax from, their employes. The
law of this State authorizes an employer
to pay the polltax of an employe who
has refused to pay the same on demand
by a deputy of the Assessor, and it aays
further that "every person who pays the
polltax of another may deduct the same
from any indebtedness to such other
person"; "but the law does not say that
any employer shall forcibly collect poll
tax from his employe. The Assessor is
allowed 15 per cent for collecting polltax.
EX-OFFICIOâ R. O. T. C, City. Unless

organization which expressly declares
that an ex-ofncio member of a commit
tee shall not have a vote he is entitled to
all the privileges of the other members
of the committee, but he should not. If
he is chairman of the organization, and
is ex-ofneio member of committees, be
made chairman of any such committee,
as it would be rather a peculiar proceed
ing for him as chairman of the commit
tee to report to himself as chairman of
the organization, for it is proper for a
committee to report to "the chairman
and members."

MERRIAM TO ARRIVE
SOON.
The General Will Leave
Vancouver Barracks This
Eveningâ War Notes.
Telegraphic advice has been received at
United States army headquarters In this
city to the effect that General H. C.
Merriam, commander of the Departments
of California and the Columbia, will leave
Vancouver barracks on the Oregon ex
press this evening, to arrive here Monday
morning.
Orders have been received at the adju-
tant-general's office of the department in
structing Lieutenant C. L. Potter of the
Engineer Corps, O. S. A., to report to
General Merritt upon hfs arrival from the
East. It is expected that Lieutenant Pot
ter will accompany the expedition to the
Philippine Islands, where his services will
be of paramount worth in the building of
fortifications, work with which he is thor
oughly familiar, having had considerable
practical experience in the building of the
fortifications at Fort Point, in San Fran
cisco harbor, where he was stationed for
some time. Lieutenant Potter arrived
yesterday.
Pursuant to telegraphic instructions
from the War Department, General Mer
rlam has detailed Captain Harry C. Ben
son, Fourth United States Cavalry, and
Second Lieutenant Charles R. Krauthoff,
Fourteenth United States infantry, to in
spect and purchase such number of horses
and mules a.s may be communicated to
them in special instructions by the chief
quartermaster of the department. Lieu
tenant Krauthoff is announced as acting
assistant quartermastor for this purpose.
The.^e officers will proceed to such place
or pl.-ices within the geographical limits
of the Department of California as i.i
then judgment may be to the best inter
est of the service to procure the number !
and kind of animals desired, and upon
completion of this duty return to their
respective stations.
It is said that about 400 horses and the
same number of mules will be purchased
at once. The price fixed for artillery
horses is $135 and tor cavalry horses $125
each. The price to be paid for mules has
not yet been decided upon.
Major Charles 11. W nipple, chief pay
master of the department of the Colum
bia and Major Charles E. Kilbourne, pay
maser T". S. A., will arrive in San Fran
cisco to-day from Portland, having been
ordered by telegraph to proceed to the
Philippines.
Major Whipple was appointed paymas
ter from civil life in 18S1, and became
chief paymaster of the department of the
Columbia in January. 1597. He Is a son of
the Episcopal Bishop of Minnesota.
Major Kilbourne graduated from West
Point in 1860. and was appointed lieuten
ant in the Second Artillery. In December,
1890 he was appointed captain In the Sig
nal Corps, and in November, 1593, he rose
to the rank of major and was made a
paymaster.

of the Fourteenth United States Infan
try did not know that his pun was loaded,

cery line in town, and when effort is

sages pertaining to the organization or
troops some housewife gets in to order a
roll of butter. . .

Major McCarthy was yesterday appoint
ed by Major White. U. S. A., as acting
brigade surgeon. He has established the
brigade hospital in the hospital tent of
the First Regiment. Serious cases will
be brought to the brigade hospital for
treatment. All of the regiments will still
maintain their own regimental hospital,
but a detail of surgeons will be made for
each day from the different regiments,
foi duty in the brigade hospital. One
or more surgeons, however, will remain
at their regimental hospital. Major Mc-
Carthy speaks in high terms of the valu
able assistance rendered by the Red Cross
Society in donating supplies to the hos
piial corps.
Rev. Father W. D. McKinnon, of St.
Alphonsus Church, at Suisun. reported to
Colonel Smith for duty yesterday. He
was assigned to quarters in Officers' Row.
Under the provisions of military law
requiring that on or before the sixth day
after enlistment, the articles of war shall
be read to the enlisted men, the same
were read to the companies In the i irst
Regiment yesterday by their company
commanders.
Lieutenant Hunt, the recruiting officer
of the Fourteenth Infantry, is experienc
ing no difficulty In getting men to fill the
vacancies in Companies I and K. In ad
dition to bringing these two companies
up to their full complement he will also
recruit men for Companies L and ~1,
which are the two new companies yet to
be formed. As soon as these are recruited
commissioned -and non-commissioned offi
cers will be assigned them.
Captain Carrtngton yesterday finished
mustering in the necessary men to fill the
vacancies existing in the Sixth and Sev
enth Regiments. There will be a regi
mental drill of the First this morning
from 8 to 10 o'clock. Yesterday was visi
tors' day at camp, but no one was al
lowed to pass the lines into the company
streets. When a soldier was wanted by
relatives and friends, he was brought
from out the camp by a sergeant, 1 nig
precaution of not allowing anyone within
the lines is in accordance with strict mili
tary discipline.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, MAY 14, 1898.
men of the battalion of heavy artillery
stationed at the Fontana Warehouse near
Fort Mason have for the past few days
experienced some of the unpleasant Inci
dents of a campaign because of a lack
of overcoats and blankets, they are now
being comfortably cared for and hence
forth will have no reason for complaint.
Yesterday afternoon Captain Long, acting
chief quartermaster of the Department of
California, issued all of the overcoats and
blankets called for in the requisition made
by Major Rice, the commanding officer of
the battalion.
Captain Long will at the earliest day
possible issue all of the other supplies
necessary to fully equip the battalion. As
soon as this is done the heavy artillery
will, in pursuance to orders heretofore
received, take station at Fort Point.
Although the men have endured dis
comfort on account of a lack of proper
clothing, splendid discipline has prevailed
throughout the whole time they have been
in quarters, and such due respect for their
officers has been shown by the men even
under the disagreeable circumstances that
they must be commended for their forti
tude.

TROOPS ARRIVE
FROM THE NORTH
Battalion of Oregon Vol
unteers Camp at the
Presidio.
The first assembly call for drill was
just sounding in the camp at the Pre
sidio yesterday morning as the First B;it
talion of the Second Regiment of Ore
gon volunteers came marching into the
Government reservation. Despite their
long trip from Portland the four com
paniesâA, B, C and Dâ presented a most
striking and soldierly appearance. Lieu
tenant-Colonel George O. Yoran was in
command of the battalion, which wasr
marched to the western end of the cav
alry parade, where they wont into camp
just beyond the Fourteenth United States
Infantry. The men are line looking sol
diers, and are a magnificent body of physi
cal manhood. Tall, strong and brawny,
with a determined look upon their coun
tenances, they will no doubt give a good
account of themselves when they arrive
at Manila.
Major C. IT. Gantenboin reported a
pleasant trip, with no accidents or sick
ness. The battalion left Portland on
Monday night and came on a .special train
over the Southern Pacific. The other
eight companies composing the regiment
will leave Portland on Monday under the
command of Colonel Summers. T^iey
will arrive here next Wednesday morn
ing, and the two battalions will go into
camp beside their fellow soldiers, thus
completing the regiment.
The four companies are officered as
follows:
Company Aâ Captain. Herbert 1... Heath,
McMinnville; first lieutenant, Ralph Platt,
Portland; second lieutenant, J. A. Young, Mc-
Minnville.
Company B â Captain, John L,. May, Ashland;
first lieutenant. Frank B. Hamlin, Roseburjf;
second lieutenant, James K. Thornton, Ash-
land.
Company Câ Captain, W. S. Moon, Eugene;
flrvt lieutenant, Robert 1,,. Huston, Kujrene;
second lieutenant, Fred W. Halnes, Roseburf.
Company I>â Captain, Austin F. .prencott. 1*&
Grande; first lieutenant, George A. Hartmun
Jr., Pendleton; second lieutenant, Frank A.
Meade, La Grande.
Company A is composed of men from
Portland and MoMinnvllle, Company B
is made up of volunteers from Roseburg
and Ashland, Company C comes from
Eugene and Company D halls from La
Grande and Pendleton.
The old Springfield rifles -with which
the men have been equipped for drilling
purposes will be exchanged for the new
Krag-Jorgensen guns.
Assistant Surgeon H. W. Cardwell and
a detail of six men of the hospital corps
accompany the battalion.
The regiment is under command of Col
onel O Summers, who will arrive next
week. He served throughout the war of
the rebellion and has been prominently
identified with the Oregon National Guard
ever since its organization. Colonel Sum
mers, who is a successful crockery mer
chant, was recently appointed appraiser
of customs for the port of Portland.
The physical examination of the Ore
gon volunteers has been very strict. That
Borne of the rejected ones were accepted
in the regular army at Fort Vancouver
is Indeed a compliment to the physical
standing of the volunteers.
This regiment has been named the, Sec
ond Volunteers of Oregon in order not to
confuse the records wit.i those of the
First Regiment of Oregon Volunteers.
which served in the war of the rebellion.
The Oregon emergency corps had on last
Wednesday raised about $1000. and it was
decided to give the captain of each com
pany $100. The emergency fund will be
increased from day to day.
Company A is commanded by Captain
H L. Heath, Company B by Captain J.
L May, Company C by "W. 8. Moon, end
Company D by Captain A. P, Prescott.
Lieutenant Ralph Platt is the auarter
master of the regiment, and Lieutenant
F. S. Kelly the adjutant. The hospital
corps 1b under the command of Surgeon-
Captain H. W. Caldwell.
The battalion has its full complement
of men, being 438 strong. There are very
few raw recruits In the regiment.
WANT TO AID
ON THE FIELD
A Quartet of Physicians
Are Accepted From
Oakland.
OAKLAND, May 13.â The spirit of pa
triotism has become so manifest among
a section of the medical fraternity of this
city that there is danger of the wounded
soldiers being better cared for than the
sick of Oakland.
A week ago Dr. WoolÃey had four com
panions in his suite of offices at Twelfth
and Broadway. One-half of them have
already gone into service at the Presidio,
and the other half have had their services
accepted and aro waiting for commissions
from the army authorities.
This morning Dr. Harry Travers and
Dr. Ijiidlow assumed their duties at the
Presidio as members of the surgeon corps
of the First Regiment. Health Officer
Dunn and Dr. O. D. Hamlin also volun
teered their services and will leave for
Manila should any cavalry regiment be
t<< -lit nut.
Health Officer Dunn has been surgeon
major of the Fifth Regiment for two
years and has organized a complete hos
pital corps in this city. One of the most
interested in this work was Dr. Hamlin.
A few days np.o Major Dunn decided that
he could be of greater service to the army
than as Health Officer of this city, and he
expressed his intention of giving up his
private practice and his official position,
each of which is very lucrative, and of
peeking active service in the army. His
offer was accepted and he was informed
that when cavalry were ordered to Ma
nila ho would receive a commission. This
spirit of the major's affected Dr. Ham
lin, who also has a good practice in this
city. Ho made a formal offer of his ser
vices and it was accepted. There is only
a partition between the offices of these
doctors and Drs. Ludlow and Travers,
and the spirit of enthusiasm which start
ed in Pr. Woolsey's office worked along
till yesterday, when Drs. Travers ana
Tjtidlnw went to the Presidio and w^re
agreeably surprised to find that they
could stand the physical examination re
quired fur service.
This morning they bade fareweJl to
their friends and ore now with the First
Regiment at the Presidio.
War Incidents.
Hesperian Parlor No. 137, N. S. G. W..
has resolved to remit all dues, fines and
assessments against any member joining
the army or navy of the United States
to fight In the war with Spain. Rincon
Parlor No. 72 has adopted a similar reso
lution.
Court Golden Gate, of the Foresters of
America, will keep in good standlnp any
of its members who will serve the Gov
ernment and defend the honor of the
country during the war with Spain.
The Ancient Order of United Workmen
in the lodges of this city is showing the
patriotic spirit as well as other organi
zations. Franklin Lodge and Crocker
Lodge have each adopted resolutions to
keep in good standing any of their mem
bers who may volunteer to serve in the
army or navy of the United States dur
ing the war.
The Labor Council.
Beyond the usual lengthy discussions
on the labor questions of the day there
was little business transacted at the meet
ing of the Labor Council last night. The
musicians reported that the Golden Eagle
Clothing House had acceded to their re
quest and in future would employ none
but union musicians. The fight with the
management of the concert hall opened
in the old Bella Union is still on. The
printers reported that meir strike was
progressing .avorably so far as they are
concerned, although the gains arp such as
are not directly noticpuble on the sur
face. A committee consisting of E. L.
Clapp J. Hill and Ed Rosenberg was ap
point* d to act in conjunction with a sim
ilar committee to be appointed from the
Tvpof?raphical Union and the Building
Trades Council to arrange for a oall for
the benefit of the striking printers.
No one will use a fountain pen after
trying a "Koh-I-Noor" copying pencil. * j
Stole Valuable Jewelry.
Last evening a sneak thief entered the
residence of Mrs. Crawford, at 6 Walnut
avenue, and stWe a package containing
several hundred dollars" worth of jewelry.
Mrs Crawford suspects a young man who
visited her house under the pretext that
he wanted to see her son.
Suit Against an Estate.
Mrs. Julie Chapman commenced suit
yesterday against Josephine P. Jaudin,
administratrix of the estate of Arnotte
Schwob. to recover $500 claimed to be due
for services rendered the decedent dur
ing her last illness.

FAST DOGS CAN
CAPTURE
GOLDEN DOLLARS.
At Union and Ingleside coursing parks
to-day the long tails will be sprinting
for purses aggregating no less than $1700.
Ihe stake at Ingleside is for dogs that
have not won third or better during the
past six months. The entry embraces
fifty-two candidates, and among them not
a few whose eligibility to a so-called non
winner contest seems incompatible with
their well-known quality. They are dogs
of class, and out of their class solely
through some of the numerous chances
that make coursing a sport of class. At
Union Park two stakes will be run offâ
an open, all-age feature with sixty-four
contestants, and a puppy event wherein
the youngsters number an even two
dozen. The candidates are for the most
cart of excellent stock. The running at
both parks will begin at 12 o'clock, and
before the finals will have been decided
to-morrow night some top notch sport
will result.
The continued success of Connemara
and Moondyne has occasioned much re
mark of late. It has been conceded that
they are a remarkably valuable brace of
performers and a glance into the pa^t
shows thc-m to be all that is claimed.
Since the first of last July Moondyne has
appeared at Ingleside sixteen times, out
of which he has won four "iirsts," one
"second," three "thirds," and only oiu-e
has failed to land his initial course. Dur
ing the same period Oonnemara has run
fifteen times at Ingleside, scoring six
â¢â¢firsts" and one "second," and failing to
win the initial run twice.
The lield at Ingleside has been further
gone over this week and is in a condi
tion almost perfect. Since the nrst of last
July and exclusive of to-day's stake
there has been disbursed in prizes at In
gleside $20,400. By the first oi" next July
the amount will have passed beyon
There bids fair to be several har
"throwdowns" at Union Park this afte
noon ThÂ« entry shows divers "stran
gers," and "strangers" at times creat

in which she was whelped. If the cur
was correct it is hard to see how she

There has been a notable change in th
personnel of the stake winners at Ingle
eide since, the opening of 189$. Of the win
ners of the all-age events between Jul
1 1897 and January 1, IS9S, the only ones
to lift a Ilnal flug since January 1 have
been Connemara, Rosette, Moondyne and
Seminole.
John Stout, the Sacramento coursing
enthusiast, has abandoned the sport, lie

J. Dean's trio, Mialmo. Moondyne and
Connemara, are being rested up for a

STRIPED BASS CAUGHT
FROM OAKLAND BRIDGE.
The Fish Commissioners' deputies-
Cross and Nelsonâ have been engaged for
days in catching black bass fry
from Russian River.
The little fishes will be placed in some
waters which are considered "just right"
for this variety of game fishes. It is
presumed, however, that the shipments
will be made to such lakes and rivers as
are considered open to the anglers of the
State. It is a well-known fact that fishes
taken from public waters cannot lawfully
be planted in waters that are under pre
serve rule, consequently it is presumed
that the fry of the bass now being ship
ped from Russian River will not find a
home under preserve rule excepting, of
course, during the close season for fish
ing.
Al Wilson, the expert angler, hooked
and landed a fifteen-pound striped bass
while he was prospecting in Russian
River for large fish recently. He reports
that the baas made a gallant fight from
start to finish.
Anglers will be pleased to learn that
during the past week Quite a number of
etriped bass have been caught by fisher
men from the Oakland bridge. Andy,
â¢'the hermit of the White House," land
ed six or seven fish, the largest of which
tipped the scales at six and three-quarter
pounds. He states that ihe most kill
ing of lures is a bunch of wiggling spile
worms, attached to which is a strip from
the bcuy of a smelt or whitefish.
It is cad that the school of young steel
heads which was "camped" In the White
House pool at Point Reyes for several
weeks, has disappeared. Doubtless the
fry thought it was about time to seek
the cool waters of Tomales Bay. Charles
Precht, James Chesley and other expert
flycasters whipped the pool last Sunday
for hours without success.
Trout fishing on the Sacramento River
near Simms is now at its best. Messrs.
Bogard and Emmons caught two nice
baskets ot rainbows last Sunday.
John Gallagher, who is "anchored" at
Baird on the McCloud River, wrote John
Butler last Tuesday that he is enjoying
excellent sport, having landed several
two, three and some five-pound trout
since hie arrival.
Is'othiaj; has been heard from Trucked
this season, but as the water is reported
very low and clear trout fishing should
soon be at its best.
Paladin!, the well-known fish merchant,
and director general of the net fishing
interests of this coast, was arrested again
last Saturday for having striped bass less
than three pounds in weight in his pos
session. Judge Low postponed a hear
ing of the case until next week. But
as the evidence against the fish dealer
is as clear as crystal, it is a -foregone
conclusion that the transgressor of the
law will have to pay dearly for his brav
ery. Still there have been very clear
cases which the Police Court Judges were
unable to see through, even with the aid
of glasses.
James Watt and a friend caught a nice
mess of trout on Lagunitas Lake last
Thursday. Watt tested his new spoon
with remarkable success. He has named
it the Brooks spinner, in honor of the
doctor.

The Field of Sport
THE BENCH SHOW
WILL OPEN
ON WEDNESDAY.
The total number of entries to the San
Francisco Kennel Club's bench show
amounts to the respectable total of 564.
This is only a few under the record-
I breaker of last year, and, considering that
the war with Spain holds the interest of
the people' at straining point, the showing
is one to be proud of. â 
In the mastiffs a young novice will be
shown by C. A. Spreckels, the vice-presi
dent of the Mastiff Club. The secretary
I has secured Major, the winner in 1595, and
believes that he has a mastiff second to
n+> tie in this country. Mrs. J. P. Norman
shows the veteran prize-winner, Lola, but
the latter is in poor condition, and it is
not at all^unlikely that Mr. Geary, the
treasurer of the club, will emerge first
; In the winning class with his promising
young bitch, Juno.
In the Great Danes some fine animals
will be shown that have heretofore been
strangers to the ring. M. E. L. Living
ston's Commodore Dewey is a promising
puppy, and the two Caesars, owned by
Mrs. J. L. Carpenter and Dr. Jelllnck, re
spectively, are both sound young. dogs. Of
' course, the great light will be in the open
class, between the two challenge dogs,
Mrs. Roncovleri's Rex R and J. L. Cun
ningham's Ivan.
In the limit bitches the palm will doubt
less fall to Mrs. B. Bryon's Queen Olga,
who won first novice at San Jose with
plenty to spare.
In rough-coated St. Bernards will be
seen some new specimens of great prom
ise, among them R. 11. Elder's Senator
Bernardo and Mrs. G. C. Oxnard's Grand
Master.
In smooth-coated St. Bernards there is
little competition.
In deerhounds Dr. McNutt's Derby and
Clinton Worden's Highland Chief are of
high quality, and the doctor's Thora 111
will doubtless bear off the palm in the
I limit class.
In the greyhound classes competition
will be keen.
Altogether for quality of the exhibits
the forthcoming show will not have many
superiors in this country, and would
probably be second to the -Westminster
Kennel Club's show In New York and
to that alone. This is an achievement of
which this coast may well boast, and one
to which sportsmen can point with pride
when discussion with the East arises.
The premium list is a marvel, and can
compare in value of prizes offered with
the largest shows ever held in this coun
try and lose nothing by the comparison.
Above all, it reflects credit not only on
the club holding the show but on the
generous civic spirit of the gentlemen who
add such munificent inducements to ex
hibitors with entire disinterestedness.
Such liberal unselfishness one fails to find
in the Eastern shows.
The specialty clubs have not shown up
as well as usual. The Pacific Mastiff Club
looms up conspicuously with its long ar
ray of specials, all of which are calculated
to foster competition.
The Oakland bench show will probably
be postponed until September. It was de
cided at the last meeting of the Oakland
Kennel Club to postpone the show, and
the consensus of opinion among the mem
bers apparently favor the autumn as the
best time for holding it.
The Pacific Mastiff Club will hold Its
annual meeting on the 20th inst. at the
Mechanics' Pavilion. For a club that has
only been in existence for one brief year
this one has had a decidedly stormy ex
istence. But. like the animal it favors,
the Mastiff Club seems to strive on strife
and is much stronger now than when it
started.

WITH THE OARSMEN
AND THE SCULLERS.
Ihe Ariel Rowing Club will hold its re
gatta on the first Sunday in June, but the
programme is not yet quite settled,
though it is certain that there will be
some open events. The Alameda Boat
Club will send over some representatives
and possibly its new barge. Crews are
now going out for practice every night.
The senior crew is in training and goes
out on alternate nights. The "German"
crew is enthusiastic and intends to give
its opponents a hard race. The other ju
nior crews are also getting into good trim.
The Club intends shortly to give an order
for a new racing skiff. At present Frank
Wilson, the senior sculler, is using Phe
lan's skiff, but he finds the sculls light,
and intends to use a longer and heavier
pair. The oars for the new four-oared
barge being built for the Ariels will be
got from the East.
The entries for the A. B. Treadwell ju
nior medal for skiffmen close at the house
of the Dolphin Boating Club at 10 o'clock
on Sunday morning, May 15. The race
will take place on the 22d. Up to the
present there are ihree entries: Victor
Caglieri, T. J. Hopkins and C. M. Far
rell. The executive committee has been
instructed to buy pulleys and exercising
machines; and in order to offer greater
attractions to members at the clubhouse
it has been suggested that a piano should
be purchased. The following constitute
the committee for the nomination of offi
cers: J. S. Earls, Elkon Coney, Joseph
Farrell, C. M. Farrell and L. Duncan.
The South End Rowing Club picnic was
quite successful, and now that it is over
members will resume rowing and crews
will be organized. It Is probable that the
manager of El Campo will arrange for a
regatta there soon to extend over two
Sundays, all the barge races being- held
on one day and the skiff races on the
other The South End Rowing Club has
promised its support and will probably
enter representatives in every race. The
club entertains high hopes of Len Hausler
winning the Junior shell race, but fears
that it may lose the services of Dr. C. C.
Dennis, who is a lieutenant in the naval
battalion.
The Triton Boat Club is in a flourishing
condition as regards its finances, the
number of its members and the condition
of Its boats and clubhouse; all bills arc
paid and there is money on hand. There
are about a dozen applicants for member
ship The election of club officers is ap
proaching; the new board of directors
will take charge next month. Orders have
been placed for new boats.
The ranks of the Alameda Boat Club
have beÂ«n depleted by the enlistment into
the army of five membersâ B. H. and J.
G Hanks, Otto Meyer and E. H. Hen
ninger, all of the First Regiment, and J.
C Stamer of Company G, Alameda. Otto
Meyer was a particularly promising mem
ber of the Junior crew. John Lewis will
probably be the club representative in
outrigged skiff races this season. The
new four-oared barge, which was chris
tened some weeks ago, is finished, and
will be ready for use to-morrow; addi
tional braces* have been put in and it Is
now a strong boat.
THE LAST DAYS OF
COLLEGE SPORT.
Mourning is the costume of the Califor
nia track team. Cheated out of a trip
East, their cherished scheme of visiting
the three large cltieS in the north and" en
gaging in athletic combat with their
northern rivals having come to naught,
they are deploring the fate that deprives
them of a vacation at the expense of the
people. Plans had ben consummated for
field days with the Multnomah, Seattle
and Tacoma athletic clubs. Expenses
had been guaranteed and everthlng was
In readiness for an early departure, when
Manager Dozier received information
from the Multnomahs that their team
was sadly crippled by the enlistment of
men in the volunteer army, and conse
quently the team would be bo weakened
that it would b% useless to compete with
THE SAIS FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, MAX 14, 189 S.
the crack Berkeley team with any hope
of victory. As the Multnomahs are the
strongest aggregation of athletic talent
in the north, and as the trip was planned
principally for this meeting. It waa
deemed best to postpone at least for a
time the intended trip.
At one time it looked as if there would
be a rupture between Garret Cocnran,
the university football coach, and the ath
letic management. Cochran, with his
Eastern ideas, expected a greater en
thusiasm In matters of football. The in
difference displayed by the students in
the preliminary practice brought about
seemingly inextricable difficulties, but an
understanding has been reached and it
is now expected thnt nothing but har
mony will exist. <'uchran will return a
few weeks before the. opening of the col
lege and reeontinue preliminary practice
for the fall season.
At Stanford the students are in the
midst of their examinations and athletics
of every kind are relegated to a top shelf.
Nothing will be done at Palo Alto until
the beginning of September.
NO MORE "HEFTING."
Treasurer Reis Petitions the Super-
visors to Purchase Coin Scales
for His Office.
Treasurer Rels Is not in favor of "heft
ing" as a means of counting the coin in
his charge, so he has petitioned the Super
visors to purchase a coin scales at a cost
of $90. for use in his department. The
Finance Committee yesterday recommend
ed the purchase.
The committee also adopted a resolu
tion empowering its members to call a
meeting of citizens to make arrangements
for the celebration of the Fourth of July.
Tlie draft of an ordinance was approved
amendatory to an order regulating the
amount of the appropriations embraced
in the general fund so Â« to permit of
demands being paid wl f, : wore now in
excess of, the several segregated funds.

The battery work of Whalen and Pet
ers of the Stockton team was the feature
of last Sunday's game in Stockton.
White, centerflelder of Stockton, set the
fans wild by his wonderful fielding, mak

the "hair's breadth order" in last Sun
day's game against Oakland.
Henry Krug's desira for his release
from the San Francisco team was dissi
pated as a result of last Sunday's game.
"Hennie" has decided to remain this sea
son with the home club, where his pitch
ing should prove an important factor in
that organization's championship aspira
tions.
Russell of the Oaklands has made good
the assertion that he can pitch. He was
particularly effective against Stockton,
with men on bases, and fields his position
beautifully. As a battery pitcher he has
few superiors.

stitute for Olympic Cyclers, San Fran
clscos and Bushnell Alert* Juniors, is of
the opinion that ball players would make
good soldiers and should be compelled to
enlist. The most remarkable thing about
"Keegan's" theories is that he can give
no reason why ball players should make
better soldiers than the ordinary creature.
It is hinted that these peculiar theories
of Harry's have been the cause of his
being placed on the substitute list. It's a
sad case. President Bert should take im
mediate action, or, as "Keegan" inno
cently says, "I will never play againâ
not if Manager Coffroth should pay me
in advance."

CYCLERS STILL
DISCUSS
THE RELAY RACE.
Although the annual relay race for IS9B
has passed and been won by the same
club for the fourth successive time, It Is
Btlll the principal topic of conversation
among the racing enthusiasts. Rumors of
protests have been rffe, but they have
( ended as they startedâ in wind. There was
also some talk about matching the Bay
City and Olympic teams to ride the dis
tance over again, this time on a track;
but the former club laughed at the propo
sition. President Kerrigan told them that
his club's team had been selected with a
view to its efficiency on the road; some
of them were not speedy track riders.
Further, he thought that the race would
develop into a case of follow-the-leader,
until the tenth relay, when there would
be a ten-mile race between Wing (Olym
pic) and Furman (Bay City), and that as
the latter was immeasurably Wing's su
perior the result would be a foregone con
clusion, and, such being the case, it would
not draw a crowd and the clubs would be
out money on the proposition.
Furman, who is a Postal-Telegraph op
erator at L,os Angeles, went back to his
key on Wednesday. He said before leav
ing- that, as Wing had plenty of time, he
could come down there and get a match if
he wanted it, -but expressed little expect
ancy that he would.

though defeated, may take up the chal

Ex-Captain Joe Catanich of the Imperial
Cycling Club regrets that his club did not
put In a team now, as he thinks it had
racing men fast enough to land their col
j ors well up to the front. The San Fran
cisco Road Club is another organization
that should have entered the race. These
ciubs will find that their racing men like
! to be represented on relay teams, and are
j likely to desert and join other clubs that
do put in teams if their own club does not.
Witness the cases of the Black brothers
of the Golden Gate, now of the Bay City,
and Bozlo, formerly Imperial, now Olym-

men denying that he said anything that
could be construed as inferring that Ben
Noonan of the Bay City team was paced

Harry A. Goddard of the Bay City
Wheelmen is in town after an absence of
eighteen months in the East. He is now
bent on encircling the globe, and will
leave for Australia on the next steamer.
Meanwhile he is receiving the glad hand
from his host of friends at the club.

The I'nion Coursing Park Association,
recognizing the bonds of fellowship in all
lines of sport, and being desirous of fur
ther cementing the same, has set aside
Sunday. May 15, as wheelmen's day. Am
ple arrangements have been made at the
grounds for the checking of wheels, free
of charge, and a careful employe will be
in constant attendance for that purpose.
Each club is invited to hold a run to
morrow, having for its objective point the
spacious coursing park near Colma. T'n
attached wheelmen, as well as club mem
bers, are cordially invited, and win he
admitted to the grounds free, If in cy
cling costume. Coursing will commence
at 11:30 a. m. The sport is already a fa
vorite one with the wheelmen, and this
attempt by the park management to pop
ularize it with them should make it doub
ly so.
The "Up-to-Date Cycling Record Book"
for IS9B, published by the Cycling Ga
zette people of Cleveland, Ohio, is to
hand, and is an invaluable compendium
of racing statistics for any one interested
in the sport, embellished with half-tone
cuts of the leading record holders of the
world. The publishers will mail a copy
to any address upon receipt of 10 cents.
More than usual interest Is manifested

Webster street wharf, Oakland at 830
o'clock on the tug Walter B. Hackett and

barking and wheeling to San Mateo
beach. A swim in the surf will whet
their appetites for one of the celebrated
Reliance chicken dinners, and as they
never do things by halves it is safe to
assume that every participant on the run
will have a whole bird.

The schedule of the California Cricket

secretaries of the Alameda, Pacific and
Placer county cricket clubs for distribu

season there will be five two-days'
matches between the Alameda and Pa
cific clubs, three of which will be played
at Golden Gate, and two on the Webster
street ground at Alameda.
The opening match of the season was
begun last Sunday on the Golden Gate
ground between elevens of the Pacific and
Alameda clubs. The Pacific team batted
first, and, aided by scores of 45 from F.
Sewell, 46 from T. W. G. Wallace, 36 from
J. J. Theobard, and 22 from J. H. Harbour,
ran up a total of 213. At the call of time
Alameda had made 89 runs for 7 wickets, i
R. B. Hogues. 42. and J. H. Spiro, 23, be- I
ing the best scores. Sewell and Harbour !
bowled best for the Pacifies, and Croll ;
and Baugh for the Alamedas. The match
will be continued to-morrow, and unless
the last three wickets can add 45 runs to
the Alameda score the champions of 1897
will bo obliged to follow on. Â«â¢*'
